{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01XArk18ynb39hd515A7Z4po","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→//! # The Rust Prelude\n     2→//!\n     3→//! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\n     4→//! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\n     5→//! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\n     6→//! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\n     7→//!\n     8→//! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\n     9→//! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\n    10→//! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\n    11→//! program.\n    12→//!\n    13→//! # Other preludes\n    14→//!\n    15→//! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\n    16→//! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\n    17→//! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\n    18→//! also define their own preludes.\n    19→//!\n    20→//! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\n    21→//!\n    22→//! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\n    23→//! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\n    24→//! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\n    25→//!\n    26→//! # Prelude contents\n    27→//!\n    28→//! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\n    29→//! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\n    30→//! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\n    31→//! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\n    32→//! It re-exports the following:\n    33→//!\n    34→//! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\n    35→//!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\n    36→//! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\n    37→//!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\n    38→//! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\n    39→//! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\n    40→//!   dropping a value.\n    41→//! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\n    42→//!   a type or value.\n    43→//! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\n    44→//!   alignment of a type or value.\n    45→//! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\n    46→//! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\n    47→//!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\n    48→//!   borrowed type.\n    49→//! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\n    50→//!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\n    51→//! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\n    52→//!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\n    53→//!   seen in trait bounds.\n    54→//! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\n    55→//!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\n    56→//! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\n    57→//! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\n    58→//!   iterators of various\n    59→//!   kinds.\n    60→//! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\n    61→//!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\n    62→//!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\n    63→//! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\n    64→//!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\n//! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\n//! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\n//! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\n//!\n//! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\n//! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\n//! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\n//! program.\n//!\n//! # Other preludes\n//!\n//! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\n//! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\n//! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\n//! also define their own preludes.\n//!\n//! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\n//!\n//! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\n//! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\n//! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\n//!\n//! # Prelude contents\n//!\n//! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\n//! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\n//! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\n//! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\n//! It re-exports the following:\n//!\n//! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\n//!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\n//! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\n//!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\n//! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\n//! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\n//!   dropping a value.\n//! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\n//!   a type or value.\n//! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\n//!   alignment of a type or value.\n//! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\n//! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\n//!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\n//!   borrowed type.\n//! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\n//!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\n//! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\n//!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\n//!   seen in trait bounds.\n//! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\n//!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\n//! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\n//! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\n//!   iterators of various\n//!   kinds.\n//! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\n//!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\n//!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\n//! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\n//!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\n//!   exported as well.\n//! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\n//! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\n//!\n//! The prelude used in Rust 2021, [`std::prelude::rust_2021`], includes all of the above,\n//! and in addition re-exports:\n//!\n//! * <code>[std::convert]::{[TryFrom], [TryInto]}</code>.\n//! * <code>[std::iter]::[FromIterator]</code>.\n//!\n//! The prelude used in Rust 2024, [`std::prelude::rust_2024`], includes all of the above,\n//! and in addition re-exports:\n//!\n//! * <code>[std::future]::{[Future], [IntoFuture]}</code>.\n//!\n//! Not controlled (user-defined) names should have higher priority than controlled names\n    76→        //    built into the language or standard library. This way we can add new names into the\n    77→        //    language or standard library without breaking user code.\n    78→        // 2. \"Closed set\" below means new names cannot appear after the current resolution attempt.\n    79→        // Places to search (in order of decreasing priority):\n    80→        // (Type NS)\n    81→        // 1. FIXME: Ribs (type parameters), there's no necessary infrastructure yet\n    82→        //    (open set, not controlled).\n    83→        // 2. Names in modules (both normal `mod`ules and blocks), loop through hygienic parents\n    84→        //    (open, not controlled).\n    85→        // 3. Extern prelude (open, the open part is from macro expansions, not controlled).\n    86→        // 4. Tool modules (closed, controlled right now, but not in the future).\n    87→        // 5. Standard library prelude (de-facto closed, controlled).\n    88→        // 6. Language prelude (closed, controlled).\n    89→        // (Value NS)\n    90→        // 1. FIXME: Ribs (local variables), there's no necessary infrastructure yet\n    91→        //    (open set, not controlled).\n    92→        // 2. Names in modules (both normal `mod`ules and blocks), loop through hygienic parents\n    93→        //    (open, not controlled).\n    94→        // 3. Standard library prelude (de-facto closed, controlled).\n    95→        // (Macro NS)\n    96→        // 1-3. Derive helpers (open, not controlled). All ambiguities with other names\n    97→        //    are currently reported as errors. They should be higher in priority than preludes\n    98→        //    and probably even names in modules according to the \"general principles\" above. They\n    99→        //    also should be subject to restricted shadowing because are effectively produced by\n   100→        //    derives (you need to resolve the derive first to add helpers into scope), but they\n   101→        //    should be available before the derive is expanded for compatibility.\n   102→        //    It's mess in general, so we are being conservative for now.\n   103→        // 1-3. `macro_rules` (open, not controlled), loop through `macro_rules` scopes.
